###begin article-title 0
###xml 132 156 130 154 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 132 156 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</span>
PCNA monoubiquitylation and DNA polymerase eta ubiquitin-binding domain are required to prevent 8-oxoguanine-induced mutagenesis in Saccharomyces cerevisiae
###end article-title 0
###begin p 1
###xml 0 16 0 16 <!--CREATIVE COMMONS-->
CREATIVE COMMONSThis is an Open Access article distributed under the terms of the Creative Commons Attribution Non-Commercial License () which permits unrestricted non-commercial use, distribution, and reproduction in any medium, provided the original work is properly cited.
###end p 1
###begin p 2
###xml 78 102 78 102 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 450 461 448 459 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pol30-K164R</italic>
###xml 591 595 589 593 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">RAD5</italic>
###xml 599 603 597 601 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SIZ1</italic>
###xml 78 102 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</span>
7,8-Dihydro-8-oxoguanine (8-oxoG) is an abundant and mutagenic DNA lesion. In Saccharomyces cerevisiae, the 8-oxoG DNA N-glycosylase (Ogg1) acts as the primary defense against 8-oxoG. Here, we present evidence for cooperation between Rad18-Rad6-dependent monoubiquitylation of PCNA at K164, the damage-tolerant DNA polymerase eta and the mismatch repair system (MMR) to prevent 8-oxoG-induced mutagenesis. Preventing PCNA modification at lysine 164 (pol30-K164R) results in a dramatic increase in GC to TA mutations due to endogenous 8-oxoG in Ogg1-deficient cells. In contrast, deletion of RAD5 or SIZ1 has little effect implying that the modification of PCNA relevant for preventing 8-oxoG-induced mutagenesis is monoubiquitin as opposed to polyubiquitin or SUMO. We also report that the ubiquitin-binding domain (UBZ) of Pol eta is essential to prevent 8-oxoG-induced mutagenesis but only in conjunction with a functional PCNA-binding domain (PIP). We propose that PCNA is ubiquitylated during the repair synthesis reaction after the MMR-dependent excision of adenine incorporated opposite to 8-oxoG. Monoubiquitylation of PCNA would favor the recruitment of Pol eta thereby allowing error-free incorporation of dCMP opposite to 8-oxoG. This study suggests that Pol eta and the post-replication repair (PRR) machinery can also prevent mutagenesis at DNA lesions that do not stall replication forks.
###end p 2
###begin title 3
INTRODUCTION
###end title 3
###begin p 4
###xml 128 131 128 131 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B1 B2 B3 B4">1&#8211;4</xref>
###xml 290 291 290 291 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B5">5</xref>
###xml 292 293 292 293 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B6">6</xref>
###xml 396 397 396 397 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B4">4</xref>
###xml 398 402 398 402 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B7 B8 B9 B10 B11">7&#8211;11</xref>
###xml 405 413 405 413 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">In vitro</italic>
###xml 558 559 558 559 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B9">9</xref>
###xml 560 565 560 565 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B12 B13 B14 B15 B16 B17 B18">12&#8211;18</xref>
###xml 645 647 645 647 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">vs</italic>
###xml 764 765 764 765 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B9">9</xref>
###xml 766 771 766 771 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B12 B13 B14 B15 B16 B17 B18">12&#8211;18</xref>
###xml 774 781 774 781 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">In vivo</italic>
###xml 924 925 924 925 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B8">8</xref>
###xml 926 928 926 928 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B11">11</xref>
###xml 1043 1059 1043 1059 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Escherichia coli</italic>
###xml 1107 1108 1107 1108 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</italic>
###xml 1158 1159 1158 1159 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</italic>
###xml 1256 1257 1256 1257 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</italic>
###xml 1311 1312 1311 1312 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B8">8</xref>
###xml 1313 1314 1313 1314 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B9">9</xref>
###xml 1412 1413 1412 1413 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B8">8</xref>
###xml 1043 1059 <span type="species:ncbi:562">Escherichia coli</span>
The integrity of DNA in the cell is under constant threat from physical and chemical agents of endogenous and exogenous origin (1-4). Reactive oxygen species (ROS) that escape the cellular metabolism are an important source of DNA damage, which has been involved in pathological processes (5,6). An oxidized guanine, 7,8-dihydro-8-oxoguanine (8-oxoG), is an abundant and mutagenic lesion in DNA (4,7-11). In vitro studies show that 8-oxoG can be bypassed by eukaryotic RNA polymerases or DNA polymerases via incorporation of dCMP or dAMP opposite to 8-oxoG (9,12-18). The efficiency of the bypass reaction and the relative incorporation of dCMP vs dAMP depend upon the DNA polymerase used and the accessory proteins (PCNA and RPA) present in the reaction mixture (9,12-18). In vivo, 8-oxoG is thought to be mutagenic, yielding GC to TA transversions, which is consistent with an incorporation of adenine opposite to 8-oxoG (8,11). To counteract the deleterious effects of 8-oxoG, living organisms have evolved robust DNA repair mechanisms. In Escherichia coli, the repair of 8-oxoG mostly relies on two DNA N-glycosylases, Fpg and MutY. Fpg is an 8-oxoG DNA N-glycosylase/AP lyase that excises 8-oxoG opposite to a cytosine, whereas MutY is an adenine DNA N-glycosylase that excises adenine opposite to 8-oxoG (8,9). Inactivation of both, Fpg and MutY results in a strong GC to TA spontaneous mutator phenotype (8).
###end p 4
###begin p 5
###xml 3 27 3 27 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 139 141 139 141 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B10">10</xref>
###xml 142 144 142 144 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B19">19</xref>
###xml 145 147 145 147 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B20">20</xref>
###xml 217 219 217 219 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B21">21</xref>
###xml 220 222 220 222 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B22">22</xref>
###xml 239 240 239 240 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</italic>
###xml 365 367 361 363 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B23">23</xref>
###xml 368 370 364 366 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B24">24</xref>
###xml 466 471 462 467 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B25 B26 B27 B28">25&#8211;28</xref>
###xml 548 553 542 547 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">RAD30</italic>
###xml 643 648 637 642 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B26 B27 B28">26&#8211;28</xref>
###xml 655 664 649 658 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ogg1 msh6</italic>
###xml 669 679 663 673 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ogg1 rad30</italic>
###xml 799 804 793 798 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B26 B27 B28">26&#8211;28</xref>
###xml 887 894 881 888 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. coli</italic>
###xml 1024 1026 1018 1020 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B26">26</xref>
###xml 1219 1221 1211 1213 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B27">27</xref>
###xml 1222 1224 1214 1216 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B28">28</xref>
###xml 1231 1236 1223 1228 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">RAD18</italic>
###xml 1241 1245 1233 1237 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">RAD6</italic>
###xml 1345 1347 1337 1339 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B28">28</xref>
###xml 1354 1363 1346 1355 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ogg1 rad6</italic>
###xml 1368 1378 1360 1370 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ogg1 rad18</italic>
###xml 1498 1500 1490 1492 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B28">28</xref>
###xml 1713 1715 1705 1707 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B29">29</xref>
###xml 1716 1718 1708 1710 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B30">30</xref>
###xml 3 27 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</span>
###xml 150 155 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Yeast</span>
###xml 887 894 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 1338 1343 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
In Saccharomyces cerevisiae, the main defense against 8-oxoG is also the base excision repair (BER) pathway initiated by the Ogg1 protein (10,19,20). Yeast Ogg1 is a functional-albeit not structural-homologue of Fpg (21,22). Ogg1 is a DNA N-glycosylase/AP lyase that excises 8-oxoG and 2,6-diamino-4-hydroxy-5-formamidopyrimidine (FapyG) from gamma-irradiated DNA (23,24). Finally, inactivation of the Ogg1 protein results in GC to TA spontaneous mutator phenotype (25-28). In addition to BER, DNA mismatch repair (MMR) and DNA polymerase eta (the RAD30 gene product) also play a critical role in the prevention of 8-oxoG-induced mutagenesis (26-28). The ogg1 msh6 and ogg1 rad30 double mutants have a synergistic increase in the rate of GC to TA mutations compared with that of the single mutants (26-28). The data suggest that MMR acts as a functional homologue of the MutY protein of E. coli. The Msh2-Msh6 complex recognizes the A.8-oxoG pair with high affinity and initiates the excision of adenine opposite to 8-oxoG (26). DNA polymerase eta is thought to efficiently promote the error-free incorporation of cytosine opposite to 8-oxoG in the course of the DNA gap-filling reaction at the end of the MMR process (27,28). The RAD18 and RAD6 genes also play a prominent role in the network that prevents 8-oxoG-induced mutagenesis in yeast (28). The ogg1 rad6 and ogg1 rad18 double mutants have a synergistic increase in the rate of GC to TA mutations compared with that of the single mutants (28). The Rad18-Rad6 complex possesses an ubiquitin-conjugating activity that catalyzes the monoubiquitylation of PCNA at lysine K164, which is required for all branches of the post-replication repair (PRR) pathway (29,30).
###end p 5
###begin p 6
###xml 141 154 137 150 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 201 202 197 198 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">R</sup>
###xml 351 449 347 433 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rad5&#916;, siz1 &#916;, pol30-K164R, pol32&#916;, rad30-D570A, rad30-HH-568/572AA, rad30-FF627/628AA</italic>
###xml 454 480 438 464 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rad30-(D570A, FF627/628AA)</italic>
###xml 725 738 705 718 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 754 756 734 736 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B31">31</xref>
###xml 851 853 831 833 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B30">30</xref>
###xml 1005 1012 983 990 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vivo</italic>
###xml 141 154 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 554 559 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 725 738 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
In the present study, we explored the molecular events that link PRR and DNA Polymerase eta (Rad30/Pol eta) to 8-oxoG-induced mutagenesis in S. cerevisiae. We investigated the impact on spontaneous CanR mutation rate of a panel of mutants affected in the formation and the recognition of the modified forms of PCNA. The following mutants were tested: rad5Delta, siz1 Delta, pol30-K164R, pol32Delta, rad30-D570A, rad30-HH-568/572AA, rad30-FF627/628AA and rad30-(D570A, FF627/628AA). Structural studies of the ubiquitin-binding zinc finger domain (UBZ) of human Pol eta suggest that mutation D570A and HH568/H572AA should primarily impair the binding of ubiquitin and the formation of the zinc-finger structure in Pol eta from S. cerevisiae, respectively (31). On the other hand, F627 and F628 are essential components of the PCNA-binding domain (PIP) (30). Our results point to the monoubiquitylation of PCNA at K164 (PCNA-Ub1) as a prerequisite for Pol eta's ability to prevent 8-oxoG-induced mutagenesis in vivo. They also suggest that Pol eta's antimutator activity requires both the binding of PCNA-Ub1 via its PIP-domain and the binding of the ubiquitin moiety of PCNA-Ub1 via its UBZ-domain. Finally, this study proposes that Pol eta and the PRR machinery can also prevent mutagenesis at DNA lesions such as 8-oxoG that do not stall replication forks.
###end p 6
###begin title 7
MATERIALS AND METHODS
###end title 7
###begin title 8
Media and growth conditions
###end title 8
###begin p 9
###xml 380 381 374 375 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">l</sc>
###xml 474 475 468 469 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">R</sup>
###xml 555 557 549 551 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B32">32</xref>
###xml 0 5 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Yeast</span>
###xml 57 62 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 148 153 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
Yeast strains were grown at 30degreesC in YPD medium (1% yeast extract, 1% bactopeptone and 2% glucose, with 2% agar for plates), YNBD medium (0.7% yeast nitrogen base without amino acids and 2% glucose, with 2% agar for plates) supplemented with appropriate amino acids and bases or YNBGal medium (YNB with 2% galactose). Supplemented YNBD medium lacking arginine but containing l-canavanine (Sigma) at 60 mg/l was used for the selective growth of canavanine-resistant (CanR) mutants on plates. Pre-sporulation and sporulation media have been described (32).
###end p 9

###xml 0 5 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Yeast</span>

###xml 744 746 740 742 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B36">36</xref>
###xml 788 793 784 789 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">RAD30</italic>
###xml 800 805 796 801 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rad30</italic>
###xml 809 819 805 815 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ogg1 rad30</italic>
###xml 924 929 920 925 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">RAD30</italic>
###xml 971 976 967 972 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">RAD30</italic>
###xml 1012 1016 1008 1012 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">LEU2</italic>
###xml 1024 1026 1020 1022 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B37">37</xref>
###xml 1242 1244 1238 1240 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B37">37</xref>
###xml 1367 1377 1363 1369 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rad30&#916;</italic>
###xml 1381 1401 1373 1385 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ogg1&#916; rad30&#916;</italic>

###xml 0 24 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</span>
###xml 1529 1536 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 1766 1790 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</span>
Saccharomyces cerevisiae strains used in the present study are listed in Table 1. All strains are haploid and isogenic to wild type (WT) strains FF18733 (MATa, leu2-3-112, trp1-289, his7-2, ura3-52, lys1-1, CAN1) or FF18734 (MATalpha, leu2-3-112, trp1-289, his7-2, ura3-52, lys1-1, CAN1). Gene deletions were performed by a PCR-mediated one-step replacement technique (33,34). All disruptions were confirmed by PCR on genomic DNA. Strains were also obtained after genetic crossing and tetrad analysis. Micromanipulation and dissection of asci were performed using a Singer MSM System (35). For biochemical detection of the modified forms of PCNA, the POL30 open reading frame was replaced at its endogenous locus by a POL30-His6-tagged allele (36). For genetic analysis of mutants in the RAD30 gene, rad30 or ogg1 rad30 deletion strains were transformed with a shuttle vector based on YIplac128, which carried WT or mutated RAD30 gene under the control of the endogenous RAD30 promoter, for integration into the LEU2 locus (37). The empty vector YIplac128 served as control. Plasmids, p990 (YIplac128-Rad30), p991 (YIplac128-rad30-D570A), p1080 (YIplac128-rad30-FF627/628AA) and p1081 (YIplac128-rad30-D570A, FF627/628AA), were as described (37). Plasmid pBU001 (YIplac128-rad30-HH568/572AA) was derived from p990. Plasmids were restricted at EcoRV, transformed into rad30Delta or ogg1Delta rad30Delta cells and Leu+ transformants were selected and accurate integration was controlled by PCR analysis (Table 1). The mutY gene of E. coli was PCR-amplified from a WT strain (AB1157) and cloned into the pRS415-GAL1 (CEN ARS LEU2) plasmid. Mutations were introduced by PCR-based mutagenesis. Details of primers, strains and plasmids are available upon request. ###begin title 14
Spontaneous mutation rates
###end title 14
###begin p 15
###xml 74 75 74 75 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">2</sup>
###xml 339 340 327 328 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">R</sup>
###xml 428 429 416 417 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">l</sc>
###xml 443 445 431 433 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B38">38</xref>
###xml 608 609 590 591 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">R</sup>
###xml 648 650 630 632 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B39">39</xref>
For each strain, 11 independent cultures were inoculated with about 5 x 102 cells in 2 ml of YPD, YNBD or YNBGal medium and grown at 30degreesC for 3 days. Cell density was measured by plating dilutions on YPD or YNBD agar plates and counting the colonies after 3 days at 30degreesC. The quantification of canavanine-resistant mutants (CanR) was determined after plating on selective medium (YNBD agar plates containing 60 mg/l l-canavanine) (38). Colonies were counted after 4-5 days at 30degreesC. All experiments were repeated independently 2-5 times. Mutation rates were determined from the number of CanR colonies by the method of the median (39).
###end p 15
###begin title 16
Mutation spectra
###end title 16
###begin p 17
###xml 68 69 68 69 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">l</sc>
###xml 164 165 158 159 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">R</sup>
###xml 466 470 460 464 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">CAN1</italic>
###xml 620 624 614 618 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">CAN1</italic>
###xml 707 709 701 703 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B28">28</xref>
###xml 710 712 704 706 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B40">40</xref>
For each strain, 32 independent cultures were grown and plated onto l-canavanine-containing plates (one culture per plate). After 3 days at 30degreesC, a single CanR mutant colony per plate was isolated and streaked onto selective canavanine-containing plates. Genomic DNA was extracted from saturated 1.5 ml YPD cultures obtained from individual colonies isolated from the canavanine-containing plates using ZymolyaseR (ICN) and the DNeasy Tissue Kit (Qiagen). The CAN1 gene was amplified by PCR from genomic DNA with 5-CAN-40 (5'CAGACTTCTTAACTCCTG3') and 3-CAN1880 (5'GAAATGTGATCAAAGGTA-ATAAAACG3'). Sequencing of the CAN1 gene (1773 base pairs) was performed using three primers as previously described (28,40). Sequence alignment and analysis were performed using the DNA-STRIDER program.
###end p 17
###begin title 18
MutY activity assay
###end title 18
###begin p 19
###xml 385 387 385 387 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">32</sup>
###xml 426 428 426 428 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B41">41</xref>
###xml 526 528 525 527 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">32</sup>
###xml 623 627 622 626 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">GAL1</italic>
###xml 638 647 637 646 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">GAL1-mutY</italic>
###xml 764 767 757 760 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">600</sub>
###xml 822 824 815 817 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B42">42</xref>
###xml 1120 1122 1100 1102 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B41">41</xref>
A 34-mer oligodeoxyribonucleotide containing a single 8-oxoG at position 16 [5'-GGCTTCATCGTTGT-8-oxoG-CAGACCTGGTGGATACCG-3'] (a kind gift of Dr J. Cadet, CEA-Grenoble, France) and its complementary sequence with an adenine (A) at position 19 were used to generate a double stranded DNA substrate with a single A.8-oxoG pair. Before annealing the A-containing complementary strand was [32P]-labeled at the 5'-end as described (41). The assay mixture (15 mul final volume) contained 25 mM Tris-HCl pH 7.6, 100 mM NaCl, 50 fmol [32P]-labeled A.8-oxoG duplex and limited amounts of cell free extract of WT cells hosting pRS415-GAL1 or pRS415-GAL1-mutY plasmid. To express the MutY protein, cells were grown at 30degreesC in YNBGal medium in absence of leucine until OD600 = 1.0. Cell free extracts were prepared as described (42). The reactions were performed at 37degreesC for 15 min. Then, 6 mul of formamide dye was added before heating at 95degreesC for 5 min. The product of the reaction (P) was separated from the substrate (S) by 20% PAGE containing 7 M urea and quantified using a Molecular Dynamics PhosphorImager (41).
###end p 19
###begin title 20
Detection of PCNA modifications
###end title 20
###begin p 21
###xml 4 9 4 9 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">POL30</italic>
###xml 74 75 74 75 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">6</sub>
###xml 99 120 99 120 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">msh6, ogg1, msh6 ogg1</italic>
###xml 125 134 125 134 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">apn1 apn2</italic>
###xml 160 167 160 167 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="T1">Table 1</xref>
###xml 210 211 210 211 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">9</sup>
###xml 411 413 405 407 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B36">36</xref>
###xml 475 478 469 472 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">His</sup>
###xml 829 831 814 816 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B36">36</xref>
###xml 729 734 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
The POL30 open reading frame was replaced at its endogenous locus by a His6-tagged allele in WT or msh6, ogg1, msh6 ogg1 and apn1 apn2 deletion mutant strains (Table 1). Exponentially growing cultures, about 109 cells, were prepared. Cells were either untreated or exposed to 0.02% methyl-methane sulfonate (MMS) for 90 min at 30degreesC. Cells were harvested and processed essentially as previously described (36). Briefly, cells were lysed under denaturing conditions, and HisPCNA and its modified forms were isolated by Ni-NTA affinity chromatography from the total extracts. Samples were separated on NuPAGEtrade mark 4-12% Bis-Tris gradient gels (Invitrogen), followed by Western blotting with polyclonal antibodies against yeast PCNA and a commercial monoclonal anti-ubiquitin antibody, P4D1 (Cell Signaling Technologies) (36).
###end p 21
###begin title 22
Two-hybrid analysis
###end title 22
###begin p 23
###xml 117 119 117 119 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B37">37</xref>
###xml 359 361 357 359 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B37">37</xref>
Analysis of protein-protein interactions in the two-hybrid system was performed in PJ64-4A, as described previously (37). Bait and prey plasmids carrying PCNA* (KK127/164RR) and fusions thereof to ubiquitin* (KKK29/48/63RRR) as well as WT and mutant (D570A) versions of Rad30 (Pol eta), either full-length (1-632) or truncated (538-632), have been described (37). The mutant (HH568/572AA) of Rad30 was constructed analogously. Selection for the presence of the bait and prey plasmids was carried out on synthetic medium lacking tryptophan and leucine (-LW), and positive interactions were scored by growth on medium further lacking histidine (-HLW) or histidine and adenine (-AHLW).
###end p 23
###begin title 24
RESULTS
###end title 24
###begin title 25
###xml 119 132 119 132 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 119 132 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
Monoubiquitylation of PCNA at K164 is required to prevent high spontaneous mutation rates in Ogg1-deficient strains of S. cerevisiae
###end title 25
###begin p 26
###xml 30 35 30 35 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">RAD18</italic>
###xml 40 44 40 44 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">RAD6</italic>
###xml 170 183 170 183 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 185 187 185 187 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B28">28</xref>
###xml 394 396 394 396 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B29">29</xref>
###xml 397 399 397 399 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B30">30</xref>
###xml 400 402 400 402 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B43">43</xref>
###xml 403 405 403 405 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B44">44</xref>
###xml 472 477 472 477 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">POL30</italic>
###xml 578 580 578 580 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B29">29</xref>
###xml 581 583 581 583 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B30">30</xref>
###xml 584 586 584 586 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B43">43</xref>

###xml 170 183 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 1587 1600 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
We recently reported that the RAD18 and RAD6 genes are important components of the cellular network that prevents 8-oxoG-induced mutagenesis in Ogg1-deficient strains of S. cerevisiae (28). The Rad18-Rad6 complex possesses an ubiquitin-conjugating activity that catalyzes the monoubiquitylation of PCNA at lysine K164, which is required for all branches of the post-replication repair process (29,30,43,44). Here, we investigated the impact of the K164R mutation of PCNA (POL30 gene product) that abolishes the conjugation of ubiquitin to PCNA and greatly reduces that of SUMO (29,30,43,44). The results show that the ogg1 pol30-K164R double mutant, like ogg1 rad18 and ogg1 rad30, exhibits a synergistic increase in CanR mutation rate compared to that of the single mutants (Table 2). In contrast, the ogg1 rad5 and ogg1 siz1 double mutants do not exhibit a synergistic increase in CanR mutation rates compared to that of the single mutants (Table 2). These results suggest that neither Rad5-dependent polyubiquitylation nor Siz1-dependent sumoylation of PCNA is involved in the prevention of 8-oxoG-induced mutagenesis in Ogg1-deficient cells. Table 2 also shows that homologous recombination does not play a critical role in the antimutagenic process at 8-oxoG, since the spontaneous mutation rate in the ogg1 rad51 double mutant corresponds to the sum of the rates in the two single mutants. These results are in agreement with the notion that only monoubiquitylation of PCNA (PCNA-Ub1) at K164 plays an important role in the process that prevents high spontaneous mutation rates in S. cerevisiae. Table 2.Spontaneous CanR mutation rate in strains that cannot undergo ubiquitin- or SUMO-conjugation of PCNA in S. cerevisiaeRelevant genotypeCanR mutation rate x 10-7RatioaWild type4.4 (3.8-5.0)1.0Deltaogg117.4 (13.4-24.8)4.0Deltarad1811.4 (8.2-18.6)2.6Deltarad512.8 (10.4-16.2)2.9Deltasiz12.4 (2.2-3.2)0.5Deltarad5151.8 (39.2-74.6)11.7Deltarad306.0 (5.0-9.4)1.4pol30-K164R3.6 (2.8-4.8)0.8Deltaogg1Deltarad1870.6 (61.4-95.0)16.0bDeltaogg1Deltarad529.8 (24.4-40.2)6.8Deltaogg1Deltasiz117.2 (13.8-20.4)3.9Deltaogg1Deltarad5166.6 (53.6-69.8)15.1Deltaogg1Deltarad3057.0 (43.2-70.6)13.0bDeltaogg1 pol30-K164R58.0 (52.2-68.9)13.2bRates of mutation at the CAN1 locus were determined from the number of CanR mutant colonies by the method of the median (39). The numbers in parentheses indicate the low and high values for the 95% confidence interval for each rate.aRatio is relative to wild type.bIndicate synergism (more than 2-fold the additivity) between ogg1 and another mutation.
###end p 26

###xml 537 544 537 544 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="T3">Table 3</xref>
###xml 604 614 604 614 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ogg1 rad18</italic>

###xml 1971 2112 1967 2108 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" textid="34"><sup>a</sup>Can<sup>R</sup> mutation rates are the product of the proportion of a specific class of mutation and the total mutation rate for each strain (<xref ref-type="table" rid="T2">Table 2</xref>).</p>
###xml 1971 2112 1967 2108 <fn xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="TF4"><p textid="34"><sup>a</sup>Can<sup>R</sup> mutation rates are the product of the proportion of a specific class of mutation and the total mutation rate for each strain (<xref ref-type="table" rid="T2">Table 2</xref>).</p></fn>
###xml 2112 2113 2108 2109 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">b</sup>
###xml 2112 2195 2108 2191 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" textid="35"><sup>b</sup>Number in the brackets is the fold induction of GC to TA relative to the WT value.</p>
###xml 2112 2195 2108 2191 <fn xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="TF5"><p textid="35"><sup>b</sup>Number in the brackets is the fold induction of GC to TA relative to the WT value.</p></fn>

###xml 2195 2277 2191 2273 <fn xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="TF6"><p textid="36"><sup>c</sup>Complex mutation is a mutation that is composed of more than one molecular event.</p></fn>

relative to the WT value.</p></fn><fn id="TF6"><p textid="36"><sup>c</sup>Complex mutation is a mutation that is composed of more than one molecular event.</p></fn></table-wrap-foot></table-wrap>
If 8-oxoG is at the origin of the high spontaneous CanR mutation rate measured in the ogg1 pol30-K164R double mutant, the spectrum of mutations should be strongly biased in the favor of GC to TA (28). To test this hypothesis, sequence analysis of CanR mutations was performed in the pol30-K164R and ogg1 pol30-K164R mutant strains (Table 3). The results show that the spectrum of CanR mutations in the ogg1 pol30-K164R double mutant is nearly exclusively composed of GC to TA (96.9%), resulting in a 74-fold increase compared to the WT (Table 3). This spectrum is very similar to those obtained with the ogg1 rad18 and ogg1 rad30 double mutants (28), which points to 8-oxoG as the cause of spontaneous GC to TA in the ogg1 pol30-K164R strain. The results also show that the overall CanR mutation rate in the pol30-K164R mutant is not significantly different from that of the WT (Table 2). However, we observe a 2.2-fold increase in GC to TA in the pol30-K164R compared to the WT (Table 3). Similarly, a 1.8-fold increase in GC to TA was observed in the rad30 mutant compared to the WT (28). Although modest, these increases in GC to TA in pol30-K164R and rad30Delta suggest that a minor fraction of 8-oxoG escape the vigilance of Ogg1 and reveal the other pathways even in Ogg1-proficient cells. ###xml 296 301 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 359 366 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 406 419 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 441 448 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 582 585 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">man</span>
###xml 644 657 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 743 756 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 796 803 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 903 916 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 1066 1079 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 1501 1508 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 1512 1525 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 1540 1553 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
The synergistic increase in spontaneous GC to TA observed in ogg1 rad18 and ogg1 pol30-K164R is most probably due to 8-oxoG. However the role of another DNA lesion with the same coding properties cannot be completely ruled out. To further confirm 8-oxoG as the cause of CanR mutations in various yeast mutants, we explored the capacity of the MutY protein of E. coli to suppress spontaneous mutagenesis in S. cerevisiae. The MutY protein of E. coli is an adenine DNA N-glycosylase that specifically recognizes A.8-oxoG pairs in DNA (8). Amongst eukaryotes, most organisms including man possess a MutY-homolog with the considerable exception of S. cerevisiae (11). Genetic and biochemical data point to MMR as the functional homolog of MutY in S. cerevisiae (26). Here, we cloned the mutY gene of E. coli in the pRS415-GAL1 plasmid yielding pRS415-GAL1-mutY, which was used to transform the WT strain of S. cerevisiae. Figure 1 shows that cell free extract of WT/pRS415-GAL1 does not exhibit detectable MutY activity. This result is in agreement with the notion that S. cerevisiae does not possess a known or unknown DNA N-glycosylase able to excise adenine opposite to 8-oxoG. In contrast, cell free extract of WT/pRS415-GAL1-mutY possesses a robust MutY activity (Figure 1). Control experiments show that the MutY activity in extracts is undetectable when WT/pRS415-GAL1-mutY cells are grown in the presence of glucose instead of galactose (data not shown). Figure 1.Expression of the MuY protein of E. coli in S. cerevisiae. WT strain of S. cerevisiae harboring p415-GAL1 or p415-GAL1-mutY was grown at 30degreesC in YNBGal supplemented medium until OD600 = 1.0. Cell free extracts were prepared and assayed for MutY activity using a 34-mer DNA duplex that contained a single A*.8-oxoG pair. Total protein concentration in extracts was as follows: WT/p415-GAL1 (4.2 mg/ml) and WT/p415-GAL1-mutY (3.8 mg/ml). S: 34-mer Substrate, P: 19-mer Product. A*: the A-containing strand was [32P]-labeled at the 5'-end.
###end p 38

###xml 72 85 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
Expression of the MuY protein of E. coli in S. cerevisiae. WT strain of S. cerevisiae harboring p415-GAL1 or p415-GAL1-mutY was grown at 30degreesC in YNBGal supplemented medium until OD600 = 1.0. Cell free extracts were prepared and assayed for MutY activity using a 34-mer DNA duplex that contained a single A*.8-oxoG pair. Total protein concentration in extracts was as follows: WT/p415-GAL1 (4.2 mg/ml) and WT/p415-GAL1-mutY (3.8 mg/ml). S: 34-mer Substrate, P: 19-mer Product. A*: the A-containing strand was [32P]-labeled at the 5'-end.
###end p 39

###xml 1075 1082 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
Table 4 shows that MutY has no significant effect on the overall CanR mutation rate in WT, rad18 or pol30-K164R strains. Although biased in favor of GC to TA, the spontaneous mutator phenotype of rad18 is most likely not due to 8-oxoG but to other endogenous DNA damage(s) or structure(s) (28,45). In contrast, the MutY protein greatly reduces spontaneous CanR mutation rate in the ogg1 single mutant, which is expected if CanR mutations are due to 8-oxoG (Table 4). Table 4 also shows that the expression of MutY only partially suppresses spontaneous mutagenesis (to the rad18 level) in the ogg1 rad18 double mutant, which confirms the impact of another class of damage at the origin of mutations in Rad18-deficient cells. Finally, the expression of MutY completely suppresses (to the WT level) spontaneous mutagenesis in the ogg1 pol30-K164R double mutant (Table 4). Therefore, both the mutation spectrum and the suppression of the mutator phenotype by MutY point to 8-oxoG as the major cause of mutations in the ogg1 pol30-K164R double mutant. ###end p 40

###xml 2510 2510 2498 2498 <graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xlink:href="gkp105f2"/>
###xml 1659 2510 1659 2498 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="F2" position="float"><label>Figure 2.</label><caption><p textid="46">Ubiquitylation of PCNA in mutator strains of <italic>S. cerevisiae</italic>. Asynchronous exponentially growing untreated (no damage) cells were allowed to grow at 30&#176;C until OD600 = 1.0. MMS-treated cells were exposed to 0.02% MMS for 90 min at 30&#176;C. Crude extracts from untreated and MMS-treated cells were prepared under denaturing conditions. <sup>His</sup>PCNA was isolated and its modifications were detected by Western blotting with anti-PCNA and anti-ubiquitin antibodies. Migration of the ubiquitylated forms of PCNA (PCNA-Ub1, PCNA-Ub2 and PCNA-Ubn) and sumoylated forms of PCNA (PCNA-S) are indicated (<xref ref-type="fig" rid="F2">Figure 2</xref>, right). Migration of molecular weight markers (kDa) is indicated (<xref ref-type="fig" rid="F2">Figure 2</xref>, left). (<bold>A</bold>) WT, <italic>ogg1</italic>, <italic>msh6</italic>, <italic>msh6 ogg1</italic><sup>His</sup>-PCNA strains untreated and MMS (0.02%) treated. (<bold>B</bold>) WT and <italic>apn1 apn2</italic><sup>His</sup>-PCNA strains untreated and MMS (0.02%) treated.</p></caption><graphic xlink:href="gkp105f2"/></fig>
###xml 137 150 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 719 724 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 968 973 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 1713 1726 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
Our genetic analysis shows that monoubiquitylated PCNA (PCNA-Ub1) is an important player in the error-free processing (TLS) of 8-oxoG in S. cerevisiae. These results point to the formation of PCNA-Ub1 in untreated Ogg1-deficient cells at presumably low but potentially detectable levels. Since 8-oxoG is not thought to be a strong block to DNA replication, the formation of PCNA-Ub1 could occur in the course of the gap-filling synthesis after MMR-dependent excision of adenine paired with 8-oxoG (26-28). Therefore, if detectable under normal growth condition, the level of PCNA-Ub1 should be higher in an ogg1 single mutant than in an ogg1 msh6 double mutant. To identify the ubiquitylated forms of PCNA (PCNA-Ub) in yeast cells, the POL30 (PCNA) open reading frame was replaced at its endogenous locus by a His6-tagged allele. The presence of PCNA-Ub was monitored using affinity chromatography-purified PCNA and Western blotting with polyclonal antibodies against yeast PCNA and monoclonal anti-ubiquitin antibody (36). Figure 2A shows that PCNA-Ub cannot be detected in untreated WT, ogg1, msh6 or ogg1 msh6 cells. This is probably due to the fact that the signal is below the limit of detection of the western blotting assay. Control experiments show the formation of mono- and polyubiquitylated PCNA (PCNA-Ubn) in all four strains after exposure to 0.02% MMS (Figure 2A). SUMO-PCNA is also observed in all strain tested either untreated or exposed to 0.02% MMS (Figure 2). Therefore, the absence of detectable PCNA-Ub in Ogg1-deficient cells does not allow conclusions about the role of MMR in the formation of PCNA-Ub during the processing of 8-oxoG. Figure 2.Ubiquitylation of PCNA in mutator strains of S. cerevisiae. Asynchronous exponentially growing untreated (no damage) cells were allowed to grow at 30degreesC until OD600 = 1.0. MMS-treated cells were exposed to 0.02% MMS for 90 min at 30degreesC. Crude extracts from untreated and MMS-treated cells were prepared under denaturing conditions. HisPCNA was isolated and its modifications were detected by Western blotting with anti-PCNA and anti-ubiquitin antibodies. Migration of the ubiquitylated forms of PCNA (PCNA-Ub1, PCNA-Ub2 and PCNA-Ubn) and sumoylated forms of PCNA (PCNA-S) are indicated (Figure 2, right). Migration of molecular weight markers (kDa) is indicated (Figure 2, left). (A) WT, ogg1, msh6, msh6 ogg1His-PCNA strains untreated and MMS (0.02%) treated. (B) WT and apn1 apn2His-PCNA strains untreated and MMS (0.02%) treated.
###end p 45

###begin p 47
###xml 47 56 47 56 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">apn1 apn2</italic>
###xml 110 123 110 123 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 125 126 125 126 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B3">3</xref>
###xml 127 129 127 129 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B46">46</xref>

###xml 311 320 311 320 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">apn1 apn2</italic>
###xml 330 332 330 332 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;7</sup>
###xml 379 383 379 383 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ogg1</italic>
###xml 385 392 385 392 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="T2">Table 2</xref>
###xml 424 428 424 428 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ogg1</italic>
###xml 433 442 433 442 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">apn1 apn2</italic>
###xml 759 766 759 766 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vivo</italic>
###xml 110 123 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
For comparison, we also His-tagged PCNA in the apn1 apn2 double mutant, another spontaneous mutator strain in S. cerevisiae (3,46). Figure 2B shows the presence of PCNA-Ubn in untreated apn1 apn2 double mutant, which is deficient in the repair of abasic (AP) sites (3). Interestingly, the CanR mutation rate in apn1 apn2 [9.4 x 10-7 (6.6-15.2)] is comparable to that observed in ogg1 (Table 2). Therefore, the comparison of ogg1 and apn1 apn2 reveals that spontaneous mutation rates and levels of PCNA-Ub in untreated cells are not correlated. This result probably reflects the properties of the DNA lesions, 8-oxoG versus AP site, at the origin of the mutational events. These data support the notion that 8-oxoG does not act as a block to replication forks in vivo whereas AP site does.
###end p 47

UBZ and PIP domains of DNA polymerase eta are required to prevent 8-oxoG-induced mutagenesis

###xml 1451 1456 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Yeast</span>
###xml 2232 2245 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
Our genetic data point to a model that involves Rad18-Rad6 and PCNA-Ub1 in a cellular process that prevents 8-oxoG-induced mutagenesis. PCNA-Ub1 could be used to specifically recruit Pol eta to perform the translesion synthesis (TLS) with error-free incorporation of dCMP opposite to 8-oxoG (27,28). Indeed, the recruitment of specialized DNA polymerases by PCNA-Ub1 is a widely accepted mechanism to explain DNA synthesis across DNA lesions that stall replication forks such as pyrimidine dimer (29,30). In order to investigate the impact of the interactions between Pol eta and PCNA on 8-oxoG-induced mutagenesis, we used mutant versions of Pol eta that are affected in the PCNA-interacting domain (PIP) or in the ubiquitin-binding zinc finger domain (UBZ) (37,47). The PIP-domain mutant used here (FF627/628AA) has already been demonstrated to be deficient in its TLS function after UVC-irradiation in vivo (48). Based on structural information, we used two types of mutant versions of Pol eta affected in the UBZ-domain (31). According to this study, mutation D570A should primarily affect the interaction between Pol eta and ubiquitin, whereas HH568/572AA should abrogate the formation of the putative zinc-finger structure, respectively (31). Whereas the D570A mutation had previously been demonstrated to be deficient in TLS and ubiquitin binding (37,47), no measurable UVC sensitivity was found for HH568/572AA indicating functional TLS (47). Yeast strains that express WT and mutant versions of Pol eta were constructed and spontaneous CanR mutation rates were determined. Figure 3A shows that the expression of Pol eta WT and mutants in rad30 cells does not significantly impact on CanR mutation rates. Figure 3B shows that the expression of Pol eta-WT and Pol eta-HH568/572AA in ogg1 rad30 cells greatly reduces, to the level of an ogg1 single mutant, the spontaneous CanR mutation rates. In contrast, the expression of Pol eta-D570A, Pol eta-FF627/628AA and Pol eta-(D570A, FF627/628AA) does not result in a significant reduction of CanR mutation rates in ogg1 rad30 cells (Figure 3B). These results strongly suggest a critical role of both the UBZ and the PIP domain of Pol eta to prevent 8-oxoG-induced mutagenesis in S. cerevisiae. They also correlate with the UVC sensitivities of the respective mutants reported previously (37,47). Figure 3.The UBZ- and PIP-domains of Pol eta are required to prevent 8-oxoG-induced mutagenesis. Strains bearing a rad30 deletion (A) or ogg1 rad30 deletions (B) were complemented by expressing a chromosomal version of Rad30-WT, Rad30-D570A, Rad30-FF627/628AA (PIP*), Rad30-(D570A, PIP*) and Rad30-HH568/572AA placed under the control of its natural promoter. Strains are described in 'Materials and methods' section and Table 1. CanR mutation rates were determined as described (Table 2). Delta ogg1 CanR mutation rate value: 17.4 x 10-7 (13.4-24.8).
###end p 49
###begin p 50
###xml 106 111 104 109 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rad30</italic>
###xml 122 123 120 121 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">A</bold>

###xml 2279 3077 2245 3037 <caption xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><p textid="52">Physical interactions between Rad30-WT, Rad30-D570A, Rad30-HH568/572AA and PCNA*, Ub*-PCNA* or PCNA*-Ub* fusions. ###xml 3077 3077 3037 3037 <graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xlink:href="gkp105f4"/>
###xml 2270 3077 2236 3037 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="F4" position="float"><label>Figure 4.</label><caption><p textid="52">Physical interactions between Rad30-WT, Rad30-D570A, Rad30-HH568/572AA and PCNA*, Ub*-PCNA* or PCNA*-Ub* fusions. ###xml 2255 2268 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
Although essential, the precise role of the UBZ domain of Pol eta in the TLS process remains unclear. Indeed, the mutational inactivation of the zinc binding ability of Pol eta-HH568/572AA was expected to abolish its ubiquitin-binding ability raising the question of whether ubiquitin binding was in fact required for TLS (Figure 3 and ref. 47). Here, we compared the capacity of Pol eta-WT, Pol eta-D570A and Pol eta-HH568/572AA to interact with PCNA and PCNA-Ub in the two-hybrid system. Figure 4 shows the interactions between the different versions of Pol eta, PCNA* and linear fusions of ubiquitin to PCNA* (Ub*-PCNA* and PCNA*-Ub*). As previously reported (37), fusion of ubiquitin to PCNA significantly enhances interaction with Pol eta in an UBZ-dependent manner. Hence, Pol eta-WT fused to GAL4-AD in combination with Ub*-PCNA* or PCNA*-Ub* fused to GAL4-BD activates the HIS3 reporter gene, whereas interactions with PCNA* alone or those involving the Pol eta-D570A mutation are too weak to give a signal (Figure 4A). Reversal of the orientation in the two-hybrid system results in coherent but overall stronger signals, where Pol eta fused to GAL4-BD in combination with PCNA* alone fused to GAL4-AD activates the HIS3 reporter (Figure 4B). Preferential binding of Pol eta-WT to the ubiquitin fusions is now observed on plates selecting for activation of the HIS3 and ADE2 reporters. Under this assay condition (-AHLW), Pol eta-D570A does not generate a detectable signal with Ub*-PCNA* or PCNA*-Ub*, whereas Pol eta-HH568/572AA and Pol eta-WT efficiently bind Ub*-PCNA* or PCNA*-Ub* (Figure 4B). We also performed these two-hybrid assays with the full-length proteins, Rad30(1-632), with identical results (ref. 37 and data not shown). These results led us to conclude that mutation D570A in the UBZ domain of Pol eta adversely affects its capacity to bind PCNA-Ub, whereas mutation HH568/572AA does not. Taken together, our data suggest that Pol eta's capacity to suppress 8-oxoG-induced mutagenesis correlates with its capacity to directly bind the ubiquitin moiety on linear Ub-PCNA fusions. They also suggest that the putative Zn-finger structure in the UBZ domain of Pol eta is not required for ubiquitin binding and for TLS of 8-oxoG in S. cerevisiae. Figure 4.Physical interactions between Rad30-WT, Rad30-D570A, Rad30-HH568/572AA and PCNA*, Ub*-PCNA* or PCNA*-Ub* fusions. Interactions were monitored in the two-hybrid system, based on fusions to the GAL4 activation (AD) and GAL4 DNA-binding (BD) domains. Truncated RAD30, comprising amino acids 538-632, either WT or mutant (D570A and HH568/572AA) were used. Mutated PCNA (PCNA*) and linear fusions of mutated ubiquitin and PCNA at the N-terminus (Ub*-PCNA*) or the C-terminus (PCNA*-Ub*) were fused to GAL4-AD and GAL4-BD ['Materials and Methods' section (37)]. Interactions were scored by growth on plates lacking histidine (-HLW) and plates lacking histidine and adenine (-AHLW). Plates were scored after 3 days at 30degreesC. (A) Rad30 (538-632) fused to GAL4-AD. (B) Rad30 (538-632) fused to GAL4-BD.
###end p 51

###xml 290 303 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
Our model suggests a competition between DNA polymerases delta (Pol delta) and eta to bind PCNA or PCNA-Ub1 during the gap-filling reaction after the MMR-dependent excision of A.8-oxoG mismatches (28). The role of Pol delta cannot be directly addressed because of its essential function in S. cerevisiae. However, one of the three subunits of Pol delta, Pol32, is non-essential and can be deleted (49,50). We therefore investigated the impact of the Pol32 protein on spontaneous CanR mutation rate in ogg1 and ogg1 rad18 backgrounds. Table 5 shows that the ogg1 pol32 double mutant does not exhibit an enhanced CanR spontaneous mutation rate, compared to an ogg1 single mutant. Interestingly, the CanR mutation rate is greatly reduced in the ogg1 rad18 pol32 triple mutant compared to the ogg1 rad18 double mutant (Table 5). We propose that in the absence of PCNA-Ub1 in the ogg1 rad18 strain, both Pol delta and Pol eta are recruited at unmodified PCNA allowing significant dAMP incorporation opposite to 8-oxoG, which explains the enhanced mutagenesis in the ogg1 rad18 strain. In the ogg1 rad18 pol32 triple mutant, the recruitment of Pol eta at unmodified PCNA could be favored because of the loss of affinity of Pol delta for PCNA in absence of its Pol32 subunit, which also possesses a PIP domain (50). Consequently, the shift in favor of Pol eta would promote dCMP incorporation and explain the reduced CanR mutation rate in the ogg1 rad18 pol32 triple mutant. The numbers in parentheses indicate the low and high values for the 95% confidence interval for each rate.aRatio is relative to wild type.
###end p 54

###begin title 58
DISCUSSION
###end title 58
###begin p 59
###xml 148 161 148 161 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 226 227 226 227 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</italic>
###xml 328 333 326 331 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B26 B27 B28">26&#8211;28</xref>
###xml 554 556 552 554 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B28">28</xref>
###xml 148 161 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
The aim of our studies is to decipher the biological network that protects the genome from the deleterious action of 8-oxoG in eukaryotic cells. In S. cerevisiae, this network involves at least four components: the 8-oxoG DNA N-glycosylase (Ogg1), the Msh2-Msh6-dependent MMR, the Rad18-Rad6 complex and the DNA polymerase eta (26-28). Ogg1 is the major player and the only one able to remove 8-oxoG from DNA, which may explain why the contribution of other partners is unambiguously assessed only in Ogg1-deficient cells. Recently, we proposed a model (28): (i) due to endogenous oxidative stress, 8-oxoG forms in genomic DNA, (ii) DNA polymerase delta in the presence of auxiliary proteins present at the replication fork efficiently incorporates adenine opposite to 8-oxoG, (iii) Msh2-Msh6 complex recognizes the A.8-oxoG pairs and initiates the MMR-dependent excision of the adenine paired with 8-oxoG, which results in the formation of a gapped-structure with 8-oxoG in a single-stranded DNA (ssDNA) region and (iv) During repair synthesis, the Rad18-Rad6 complex promotes the recruitment of Pol eta, which incorporates a cytosine across from the lesion, preventing mutation fixation and regenerating the 8-oxoG.C pair substrate of Ogg1. Although attractive, this model presents a major caveat since it does not provide information about the molecular mechanisms that allow the specific recruitment of Pol eta at the site of the lesion.
###end p 59
###begin p 60
###xml 39 55 39 55 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ogg1 pol30-K164R</italic>
###xml 120 121 120 121 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">R</sup>
###xml 248 249 248 249 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">R</sup>
###xml 271 287 271 287 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ogg1 pol30-K164R</italic>
###xml 319 320 319 320 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">R</sup>
###xml 334 350 334 350 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ogg1 pol30-K164R</italic>
###xml 415 416 415 416 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">R</sup>
###xml 434 450 434 450 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ogg1 pol30-K164R</italic>
###xml 809 811 809 811 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B29">29</xref>
###xml 812 814 812 814 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B30">30</xref>
###xml 844 848 844 848 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rad5</italic>
###xml 853 857 853 857 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">siz1</italic>
###xml 874 878 874 878 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ogg1</italic>
In the present study, we show that the ogg1 pol30-K164R double mutant exhibits a synergistic increase in spontaneous CanR mutation rate, compared to the single mutants. Our results unambiguously point to 8-oxoG as the primary cause for the high CanR mutation rate in the ogg1 pol30-K164R strain: (i) the spectrum of CanR mutations in ogg1 pol30-K164R is nearly exclusively composed of GC to TA and (ii) the high CanR mutation rate in ogg1 pol30-K164R is reduced to the WT level upon expression of the bacterial MutY protein. These data led us to conclude that modification of PCNA at lysine K164 is a critical component of the cellular network that prevents 8-oxoG-induced mutagenesis. In fact, the K164R mutation of PCNA abolishes the formation of all kinds of PCNA-Ub and greatly reduces that of PCNA-SUMO (29,30). Here, we show that neither rad5 nor siz1 synergizes with ogg1, leading us to conclude that neither polyubiquitylated PCNA nor SUMO-PCNA is critical to prevent mutations at 8-oxoG. On the other hand, they strongly suggest that monoubiquitylation of PCNA (PCNA-Ub1) is essential.
###end p 60
###begin p 61
###xml 232 236 232 236 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ogg1</italic>
###xml 288 304 288 304 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">apn1 apn2 mutant</italic>
###xml 358 362 358 362 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ogg1</italic>
###xml 585 594 585 594 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">apn1 apn2</italic>
###xml 749 750 749 750 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B3">3</xref>
###xml 967 972 967 972 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B26 B27 B28">26&#8211;28</xref>
Our genetic analysis points to the formation of PCNA-Ub1 in the course of the error free processing (TLS) of 8-oxoG. To investigate this issue at the biochemical level, we measured by western blotting the formation of PCNA-Ub in an ogg1 deletion mutant. For comparison, we also tested an apn1 apn2 mutant, unable to repair AP sites. Unfortunately, untreated ogg1 cells do not exhibit detectable amounts of PCNA-Ub, like WT cells, which do not allow us to provide further information about the mechanisms of formation of PCNA-Ub1 during the processing of 8-oxoG. In contrast, untreated apn1 apn2 cells present detectable level of PCNA-Ubn. These results may suggest that 8-oxoG does not efficiently stall replication forks, whereas the AP site does (3). This is in favor of a model where PCNA-Ub1 forms during the gap-filling reaction after the MMR-dependent excision of adenine opposite to 8-oxoG. Indeed, this model relies on consistent but circumstantial evidence (26-28) and its remains possible that the role of PCNA-Ub1 and Pol eta also occurs at the stage of replication. The impact of 8-oxoG on DNA replication is also probably modulated by the sequence context around the lesion.
###end p 61
###begin p 62
###xml 14 22 14 22 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 27 34 27 34 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vivo</italic>
###xml 176 181 176 181 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B12 B13 B14 B15 B16 B17 B18">12&#8211;18</xref>
###xml 182 187 182 187 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B26 B27 B28">26&#8211;28</xref>
###xml 385 387 385 387 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B51">51</xref>
###xml 590 592 588 590 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B52">52</xref>
###xml 990 998 980 988 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 1097 1099 1087 1089 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B27">27</xref>
However, most in vitro and in vivo studies support the notion that the majority of the error-free TLS events at 8-oxoG occur in the course of a gap-filling reaction after MMR (12-18,26-28). MMR-dependent excision of adenine opposite to 8-oxoG results in the formation of stretches of ssDNA coated with RPA that might be used to activate Rad18-Rad6 and cause the formation of PCNA-Ub1 (51). However, it is potentially deleterious (mutagenic) to trigger the formation of PCNA-Ub1 for the recruitment of Pol eta during the repair synthesis after the processing of any mismatch subject to MMR (52). To reconcile these two notions, one should conclude that 8-oxoG in ssDNA specifically initiates the molecular cascade that results in the formation of PCNA-Ub1. Clearly, we suggest that Pol delta efficiently bypasses 8-oxoG in 'replication mode', but may stall or pause at the same lesion in 'gap-filling-repair mode'. It should be noted that purified Pol delta poorly replicates through 8-oxoG in vitro showing two strong stall sites, one right before the lesion and the other opposite to the lesion (27). In this model, Pol delta would initiate the gap-filling reaction and stall (pause) at 8-oxoG, thus allowing the recruitment of Rad18-Rad6 and the formation PCNA-Ub1.
###end p 62
###begin p 63
###xml 345 354 339 348 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F3">Figures 3</xref>
###xml 359 360 353 354 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F4">4</xref>
###xml 363 365 357 359 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B37">37</xref>
###xml 600 607 592 599 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vivo</italic>
###xml 609 611 601 603 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B47">47</xref>
###xml 744 757 734 747 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 759 761 749 751 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B47">47</xref>
###xml 936 938 924 926 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B47">47</xref>
###xml 1124 1133 1110 1119 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F3">Figures 3</xref>
###xml 1388 1401 1372 1385 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 1499 1512 1481 1494 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 1700 1702 1680 1682 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B31">31</xref>
###xml 1789 1802 1769 1782 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 1804 1806 1784 1786 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">CC</bold>
###xml 1818 1819 1798 1799 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</bold>
###xml 1821 1822 1801 1802 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">C</bold>
###xml 1917 1930 1895 1908 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 2021 2023 1999 2001 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B53">53</xref>
###xml 2242 2255 2218 2231 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 2495 2497 2469 2471 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B54">54</xref>
###xml 744 757 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 1388 1401 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 1499 1512 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 1618 1623 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 1661 1666 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 1789 1802 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 1831 1836 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 1917 1930 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 2242 2255 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 2381 2386 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
Finally, Pol eta binds to PCNA-Ub1 and catalyzes the incorporation of dCMP opposite to 8-oxoG. Here, we show that the UBZ of Pol eta is essential to prevent 8-oxoG-induced mutagenesis, but only in conjunction with a functional PIP motif. This notion is based on the analysis of the properties of the D570A mutation in the UBZ domain of Pol eta (Figures 3 and 4) (37). However, this notion appeared to be at variance with the conclusions of another study reporting that mutations in the zinc-binding motif of the UBZ domain of Pol eta such as CC552/553AA or HH568/572AA do not impair its TLS function in vivo (47), leading to the concept that the direct binding of the ubiquitin moiety on PCNA via the UBZ was not required for TLS by Pol eta in S. cerevisiae (47). According to this study, the invalidation of the zinc-finger structure in the UBZ domain of Pol eta should necessarily result in the loss of its ubiquitin-binding ability (47). Here we show that Pol eta -HH568/572AA not only exhibits functional TLS of 8-oxoG, but is also capable of productively interacting with the ubiquitin moiety of PCNA-Ub1, like the WT (Figures 3 and 4). Even thought ubiquitin is not attached to its natural position (K164) on PCNA, our data strongly suggest that the zinc-finger structure of the UBZ domain of Pol eta is not required for binding to the ubiquitin moiety of PCNA and hence for TLS in S. cerevisiae. It should be noted that the presence of a Zn-finger structure in the UBZ domain of Pol eta from S. cerevisiae is not firmly demonstrated: (i) to the best of our knowledge, there is no structure of the UBZ domain of yeast Pol eta (there is in fact one of the human UBZ domain, which does bind Zn) (31) and (ii) the sequence is poorly conserved, since the two cysteines are contiguous in S. cerevisiae (CCKY) versus (CEKC) in the human protein. Indeed, we cannot exclude the possibility that Pol eta's UBZ domain in S. cerevisiae does not harbor any zinc at all, in analogy to the RING-like, but zinc-less U-box domain (53). Taken together, our results are compatible with the hypothesis that points to an essential role of PCNA-Ub1 and its recognition by the UBZ and PIP domains of Pol eta to prevent UVC- and 8-oxoG-induced mutagenesis in S. cerevisiae. These conclusions may or may not be applicable to mammalian system where mutation in the Zn-finger of the UBZ domain of the human Pol eta results in different phenotypes, since the H654A is TLS-deficient, whereas H650A is TLS-proficient (54).
###end p 63
###begin p 64
###xml 695 708 679 692 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 970 972 954 956 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B55">55</xref>
###xml 695 708 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
To summarize, our current model for the late steps of the error-free bypass of 8-oxoG is as follows: (i) Pol delta in the 'gap-filling-repair' mode transiently stalls at 8-oxoG, (ii) Rad18-Rad6 is recruited at RPA-coated ssDNA allowing the formation of PCNA-Ub1, (iii) Pol eta through its UBZ and PIP domains binds to PCNA-Ub1, (iv) Pol eta preferentially incorporates dCMP opposite to 8-oxoG, (v) PCNA is deubiquitylated allowing the release of Pol eta. When one of the components of the fidelity system that favors the recruitment of Pol eta is missing the equilibrium is shifted in favor of Pol delta, which will finally lead to the mutagenic incorporation of dAMP opposite to the lesion. In S. cerevisiae, the complexity of the network orchestrated by Ogg1 points to 8-oxoG as a major cellular threat. In mammals, the inactivation of two major components of the network, that prevents mutation at 8-oxoG, namely Ogg1 and Myh1, results in a high incidence of cancer (55).
###end p 64
